width:32.511ex; height:10.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Dividing <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Delta _{1}-\\Delta _{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Delta _{1}-\\Delta _{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\Delta _{1}-\\Delta _{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6196a9adfe479c6bb9bef622ecf2d32daf4e6713" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.821ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> by the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> &#955;, the fringe shift <i>n</i> is found:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle n={\\frac {\\Delta _{1}-\\Delta _{2}}{\\lambda }}\\approx {\\frac {2Lv^{2}}{\\lambda c^{2}}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>&#955;<!-- &#955; --></mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n={\\frac {\\Delta _{1}-\\Delta _{2}}{\\lambda }}\\approx {\\frac {2Lv^{2}}{\\lambda c^{2}}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle n={\\frac {\\Delta _{1}-\\Delta _{2}}{\\lambda }}\\approx {\\frac {2Lv^{2}}{\\lambda c^{2}}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3c83249a9298099dc855663077b6a8e681bc736f" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:23.659ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Since <i>L</i>&#160;&#8776;&#160;11 meters and &#955;&#8776;500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanometer" title="Nanometer">nanometers</a>, the expected <a href="/wiki/Fringe_shift" title="Fringe shift">fringe shift</a> was <i>n</i> &#8776;0.44. So the result would be a delay in one of the light beams that could be detected when the beams were recombined through interference. Any slight change in the spent time would then be observed as a shift in the positions of the interference fringes. The negative result led Michelson to the conclusion that there is no measurable aether drift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-michel2_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-michel2-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observer_comoving_with_the_interferometer">Observer comoving with the interferometer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Observer comoving with the interferometer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>If the same situation is described from the view of an observer co-moving with the interferometer, then the effect of aether wind is similar to the effect experienced by a swimmer, who tries to move with velocity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle c}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="c" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/86a67b81c2de995bd608d5b2df50cd8cd7d92455" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.007ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> against a river flowing with velocity <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle v}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>v</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle v}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="v" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e07b00e7fc0847fbd16391c778d65bc25c452597" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.128ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teller_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-teller-28">[A 17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the longitudinal direction the swimmer first moves upstream, so his velocity is diminished due to the river flow to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle c-v}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>v</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle c-v}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="c-v" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e1719a856110eb713158906380c78b5029cc691" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:4.975ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. On his way back moving downstream, his velocity is increased to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle c+v}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>v</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle c+v}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="c+v" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8a2e47a9854cbb6ec3d79afbac9eae4d69237295" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:4.975ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. This gives the beam travel times <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle T_{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle T_{1}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="T_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2f304724948a3ef606c4a92459e22b87a954d993" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.412ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> and  <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle T_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle T_{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="T_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d1ba5f12fbb0ff766aec6e22148b429373608555" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.412ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> as mentioned above.\n</p><p>In the transverse direction, the swimmer has to compensate for the river flow by moving at a certain angle against the flow direction, in order to sustain his exact transverse direction of motion and to reach the other side of the river at the correct location. This diminishes his speed to <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {c^{2}-v^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {c^{2}-v^{2}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\sqrt {c^{2}-v^{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fb677da5b47c53aeec3a6da945386e7e9af1850a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:9.407ex; height:3.509ex;"/></span>, and gives the beam travel time <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle T_{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>3</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle T_{3}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="T_{3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac0936b6ba76bf0d900bb7315c99f64c5376f5ed" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.412ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> as mentioned above.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mirror_reflection">Mirror reflection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mirror reflection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The classical analysis predicted a relative phase shift between the longitudinal and transverse beams which in Michelson and Morley\'s apparatus should have been readily measurable. What is not often appreciated (since there was no means of measuring it), is that motion through the hypothetical aether should also have caused the two beams to diverge as they emerged from the interferometer by about 10<sup>&#8722;8</sup> radians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schum94_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-schum94-29">[A 18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For an apparatus in motion, the classical analysis requires that the beam-splitting mirror be slightly offset from an exact 45&#176; if the longitudinal and transverse beams are to emerge from the apparatus exactly superimposed. In the relativistic analysis, Lorentz-contraction of the beam splitter in the direction of motion causes it to become more perpendicular by precisely the amount necessary to compensate for the angle discrepancy of the two beams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schum94_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-schum94-29">[A 18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Length_contraction_and_Lorentz_transformation">Length contraction and Lorentz transformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Length contraction and Lorentz transformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_special_relativity" title="History of special relativity">History of special relativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Lorentz_transformations" title="History of Lorentz transformations">History of Lorentz transformations</a></div>\n<p>A first step to explaining the Michelson and Morley experiment\'s null result was found in the <a href="/wiki/Length_contraction" title="Length contraction">FitzGerald&#8211;Lorentz contraction hypothesis</a>, now simply called length contraction or Lorentz contraction, first proposed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_FitzGerald" title="George FitzGerald">George FitzGerald</a> (1889) and <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">Hendrik Lorentz</a> (1892).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lorentz95_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-lorentz95-30">[A 19]</a></sup> According to this law all objects physically contract by <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle L/\\gamma }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle L/\\gamma }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="L/\\gamma " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d76a5d677737991bcb24e7cb890454e4218b10f5" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.008ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> along the line of motion (originally thought to be relative to the aether), <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\gamma =1/{\\sqrt {1-v^{2}/c^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\gamma =1/{\\sqrt {1-v^{2}/c^{2}}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\gamma =1/{\\sqrt {1-v^{2}/c^{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a0efffd10b8047241e9d9f4498b77760af9457bd" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:18.418ex; height:4.843ex;"/></span> being the <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_factor" title="Lorentz factor">Lorentz factor</a>. This hypothesis was partly motivated by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Heaviside" title="Oliver Heaviside">Oliver Heaviside</a>\'s discovery in 1888, that electrostatic fields are contracting in the line of motion. But since there was no reason at that time to assume that binding forces in matter are of electric origin, length contraction of matter in motion with respect to the aether was considered an <a href="/wiki/Ad_hoc_hypothesis" title="Ad hoc hypothesis">Ad hoc hypothesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIMiller_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-AIMiller-14">[A 9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>If length contraction of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle L}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>L</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle L}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="L" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/103168b86f781fe6e9a4a87b8ea1cebe0ad4ede8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.583ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is inserted into the above formula for <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle T_{l}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>l</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle T_{l}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="T_{l}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0dc7062014f0e0f99427475fb2ef299e2a63b0d4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.08ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, then the light propagation time in the longitudinal direction becomes equal to that in the transverse direction:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle T_{l}={\\frac {2L{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}{c}}{\\frac {1}{1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}={\\frac {2L}{c}}{\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}=T_{t}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>l</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mrow>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>L</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mi>c</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msup>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>t</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle T_{l}={\\frac {2L{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}{c}}{\\frac {1}{1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}={\\frac {2L}{c}}{\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}=T_{t}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle T_{l}={\\frac {2L{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}{c}}{\\frac {1}{1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}={\\frac {2L}{c}}{\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {1-{\\frac {v^{2}}{c^{2}}}}}}=T_{t}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0d1bb7a57d012017463213f2c074bb047c1a55ab" style="vertical-align: -4.671ex; width:46.445ex; height:10.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>However, length contraction is only a special case of the more general relation, according to which the transverse length is larger than the longitudinal length by the ratio <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\gamma }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\gamma }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\gamma " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a223c880b0ce3da8f64ee33c4f0010beee400b1a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.262ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. This can be achieved in many ways. If <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle L_{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle L_{1}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="L_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0e79dc1b001f8b923df475ed14de023cbc456013" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.637ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the moving longitudinal length and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle L_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle L_{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="L_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c6a952cfe42c86b7741f55a817da0e251793a358" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.637ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> the moving transverse length, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle L\'_{1}=L\'_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msubsup>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msubsup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msubsup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle L\'_{1}=L\'_{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="L\'_{1}=L\'_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/af3d6f4834b9405fd82ff5592109a4ddd6084008" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:8.373ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> being the rest lengths, then it is given:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lorentz04_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-lorentz04-31">[A 20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {L_{2}}{L_{1}}}={\\frac {L\'_{2}}{\\phi }}\\left/{\\frac {L\'_{1}}{\\gamma \\phi }}\\right.=\\gamma .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msub>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msub>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msubsup>\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mo fence="true" stretchy="true" symmetric="true">/</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<msubsup>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msubsup>\n<mrow>\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; --></mi>\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo fence="true" stretchy="true" symmetric="true"></mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; --></mi>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\frac {L_{2}}{L_{1}}}={\\frac {L\'_{2}}{\\phi }}\\left/{\\frac {L\'_{1}}{\\gamma \\phi }}\\right.=\\gamma .}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {L_{2}}{L_{1}}}={\\frac {L\'_{2}}{\\phi }}\\left/{\\frac {L\'_{1}}{\\gamma \\phi }}\\right.=\\gamma .}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/01c35eee36d5c683ac5c86569c781e69c136b305" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:21.349ex; height:6.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\phi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\phi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/72b1f30316670aee6270a28334bdf4f5072cdde4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.385ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> can be arbitrarily chosen, so there are infinitely many combinations to explain the Michelson&#8211;Morley null result. For instance, if <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\phi =1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi =1}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\phi =1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fbeaac176545364b710d70d6cb83b314eb9cc4f3" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:5.646ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> the relativistic value of length contraction of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle L_{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle L_{1}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="L_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0e79dc1b001f8b923df475ed14de023cbc456013" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.637ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> occurs, but if <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\phi =1/\\gamma }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi =1/\\gamma }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\phi =1/\\gamma " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3044762ce5adb1e2d62a0b29058b376f2c52a01c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.071ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> then no length contraction but an elongation of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle L_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>L</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle L_{2}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="L_{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c6a952cfe42c86b7741f55a817da0e251793a358" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.637ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> occurs. This hypothesis was later extended by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Larmor" title="Joseph Larmor">Joseph Larmor</a> (1897), Lorentz (1904) and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincar&#233;">Henri Poincar&#233;</a> (1905), who developed the complete <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_transformation" title="Lorentz transformation">Lorentz transformation</a> including <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">time dilation</a> in order to explain the <a href="/wiki/Trouton%E2%80%93Noble_experiment" title="Trouton&#8211;Noble experiment">Trouton&#8211;Noble experiment</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Experiments_of_Rayleigh_and_Brace" title="Experiments of Rayleigh and Brace">Experiments of Rayleigh and Brace</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kaufmann%E2%80%93Bucherer%E2%80%93Neumann_experiments" title="Kaufmann&#8211;Bucherer&#8211;Neumann experiments">Kaufmann\'s experiments</a>. It has the form\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle x\'=\\gamma \\phi (x-vt),\\ y\'=\\phi y,\\ z\'=\\phi z,\\ t\'=\\gamma \\phi \\left(t-{\\frac {vx}{c^{2}}}\\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msup>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; --></mi>\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>x</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mtext>&#160;</mtext>\n<msup>\n<mi>y</mi>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n<mi>y</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mtext>&#160;</mtext>\n<msup>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n<mi>z</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mtext>&#160;</mtext>\n<msup>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo>&#8242;</mo>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>&#947;<!-- &#947; --></mi>\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>v</mi>\n<mi>x</mi>\n</mrow>\n<msup>\n<mi>c</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle x\'=\\gamma \\phi (x-vt),\\ y\'=\\phi y,\\ z\'=\\phi z,\\ t\'=\\gamma \\phi \\left(t-{\\frac {vx}{c^{2}}}\\right)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle x\'=\\gamma \\phi (x-vt),\\ y\'=\\phi y,\\ z\'=\\phi z,\\ t\'=\\gamma \\phi \\left(t-{\\frac {vx}{c^{2}}}\\right)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b9f31a735af4ae2aa9727fcdabb3b0df5dad9afa" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:53.435ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>It remained to define the value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\phi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\phi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/72b1f30316670aee6270a28334bdf4f5072cdde4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.385ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>, which was shown by Lorentz (1904) to be unity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lorentz04_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-lorentz04-31">[A 20]</a></sup> In general, Poincar&#233; (1905)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-poincare05_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-poincare05-32">[A 21]</a></sup> demonstrated that only <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\phi =1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi =1}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\phi =1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fbeaac176545364b710d70d6cb83b314eb9cc4f3" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:5.646ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> allows this transformation to form a <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_group" title="Lorentz group">group</a>, so it is the only choice compatible with the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_relativity" title="Principle of relativity">principle of relativity</a>, <i>i.e.</i> making the stationary aether undetectable. Given this, length contraction and time dilation obtain their exact relativistic values.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_relativity">Special relativity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Special relativity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> formulated the theory of <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">special relativity</a> by 1905, deriving the Lorentz transformation and thus length contraction and time dilation from the relativity postulate and the constancy of the speed of light, thus removing the <i>ad hoc</i> character from the contraction hypothesis. Einstein emphasized the <a href="/wiki/Kinematics" title="Kinematics">kinematic</a> foundation of the theory and the modification of the notion of space and time, with the stationary aether no longer playing any role in his theory. He also pointed out the group character of the transformation. Einstein was motivated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_theory_of_electromagnetism" title="Maxwell\'s theory of electromagnetism">Maxwell\'s theory of electromagnetism</a> (in the form as it was given by Lorentz in 1895) and the lack of evidence for the <a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_aether" title="Luminiferous aether">luminiferous aether</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-einstein_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-einstein-33">[A 22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This allows a more elegant and intuitive explanation of the Michelson-Morley null result. In a comoving frame the null result is self-evident, since the apparatus can be considered as at rest in accordance with the relativity principle, thus the beam travel times are the same. In a frame relative to which the apparatus is moving, the same reasoning applies as described above in "Length contraction and Lorentz transformation", except the word "aether" has to be replaced by "non-comoving inertial frame". Einstein wrote in 1916:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-einstein2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-einstein2-34">[A 23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Although the estimated difference between these two times is exceedingly small, Michelson and Morley performed an experiment involving interference in which this difference should have been clearly detectable. But the experiment gave a negative result &#8212; a fact very perplexing to physicists. Lorentz and FitzGerald rescued the theory from this difficulty by assuming that the motion of the body relative to the &#230;ther produces a contraction of the body in the direction of motion, the amount of contraction being just sufficient to compensate for the difference in time mentioned above. Comparison with the discussion in Section 11 shows that also from the standpoint of the theory of relativity this solution of the difficulty was the right one. But on the basis of the theory of relativity the method of interpretation is incomparably more satisfactory. According to this theory there is no such thing as a "specially favoured" (unique) co-ordinate system to occasion the introduction of the &#230;ther-idea, and hence there can be no &#230;ther-drift, nor any experiment with which to demonstrate it. Here the contraction of moving bodies follows from the two fundamental principles of the theory, without the introduction of particular hypotheses; and as the prime factor involved in this contraction we find, not the motion in itself, to which we cannot attach any meaning, but the motion with respect to the body of reference chosen in the particular case in point. Thus for a co-ordinate system moving with the earth the mirror system of Michelson and Morley is not shortened, but it is shortened for a co-ordinate system which is at rest relatively to the sun.</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite>Albert Einstein, 1916</cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The extent to which the null result of the Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment influenced Einstein is disputed. Alluding to some statements of Einstein, many historians argue that it played no significant role in his path to special relativity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stachel_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-stachel-35">[A 24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polanyi_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polanyi-36">[A 25]</a></sup> while other statements of Einstein probably suggest that he was influenced by it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dongen_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-dongen-37">[A 26]</a></sup> In any case, the null result of the Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment helped the notion of the constancy of the speed of light gain widespread and rapid acceptance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stachel_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-stachel-35">[A 24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was later shown by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Howard_Percy_Robertson" title="Howard Percy Robertson">Howard Percy Robertson</a> (1949) and others<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rob_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-rob-6">[A 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sexl_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-sexl-38">[A 27]</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Test_theories_of_special_relativity" title="Test theories of special relativity">Robertson&#8211;Mansouri&#8211;Sexl test theory</a>), that it is possible to derive the Lorentz transformation entirely from the combination of three experiments. First, the Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment showed that the speed of light is independent of the <i>orientation</i> of the apparatus, establishing the relationship between longitudinal (&#946;) and transverse (&#948;) lengths. Then in 1932, Roy Kennedy and Edward Thorndike modified the Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment by making the path lengths of the split beam unequal, with one arm being very short.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KennedyThorndike_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-KennedyThorndike-39">[8]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kennedy%E2%80%93Thorndike_experiment" title="Kennedy&#8211;Thorndike experiment">Kennedy&#8211;Thorndike experiment</a> took place for many months as the Earth moved around the sun. Their negative result showed that the speed of light is independent of the <i>velocity</i> of the apparatus in different inertial frames. In addition it established that besides length changes, corresponding time changes must also occur, i.e. it established the relationship between longitudinal lengths (&#946;) and time changes (&#945;). So both experiments do not provide the individual values of these quantities. This uncertainty corresponds to the undefined factor <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\phi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\phi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/72b1f30316670aee6270a28334bdf4f5072cdde4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.385ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> as described above. It was clear due to theoretical reasons (the <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_group" title="Lorentz group">group character</a> of the Lorentz transformation as required by the relativity principle) that the individual values of length contraction and time dilation must assume their exact relativistic form. But a direct measurement of one of these quantities was still desirable to confirm the theoretical results. This was achieved by the <a href="/wiki/Ives%E2%80%93Stilwell_experiment" title="Ives&#8211;Stilwell experiment">Ives&#8211;Stilwell experiment</a> (1938), measuring &#945; in accordance with time dilation. Combining this value for &#945; with the Kennedy&#8211;Thorndike null result shows that &#946; must assume the value of relativistic length contraction. Combining &#946; with the Michelson&#8211;Morley null result shows that &#948; must be zero. Therefore, the Lorentz transformation with <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\phi =1}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#981;<!-- &#981; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi =1}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\phi =1" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fbeaac176545364b710d70d6cb83b314eb9cc4f3" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:5.646ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is an unavoidable consequence of the combination of these three experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rob_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-rob-6">[A 3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Special relativity is generally considered the solution to all negative aether drift (or <a href="/wiki/Isotropy" title="Isotropy">isotropy</a> of the speed of light) measurements, including the Michelson&#8211;Morley null result. Many high precision measurements have been conducted as <a href="/wiki/Tests_of_special_relativity" title="Tests of special relativity">tests of special relativity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_searches_for_Lorentz_violation" title="Modern searches for Lorentz violation">modern searches for Lorentz violation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrino</a> sector, all of them confirming relativity.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Incorrect_alternatives">Incorrect alternatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Incorrect alternatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As mentioned above, Michelson initially believed that his experiment would confirm Stokes\' theory, according to which the aether was fully dragged in the vicinity of the earth (see <a href="/wiki/Aether_drag_hypothesis" title="Aether drag hypothesis">Aether drag hypothesis</a>). However, complete aether drag contradicts the observed <a href="/wiki/Aberration_of_light" title="Aberration of light">aberration of light</a> and was contradicted by other experiments as well. In addition, Lorentz showed in 1886 that Stokes\'s attempt to explain aberration is contradictory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jan_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Jan-8">[A 5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittaker_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Whittaker-7">[A 4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Furthermore, the assumption that the aether is not carried in the vicinity, but only <i>within</i> matter, was very problematic as shown by the <a href="/wiki/Hammar_experiment" title="Hammar experiment">Hammar experiment</a> (1935). Hammar directed one leg of his interferometer through a heavy metal pipe plugged with lead. If aether were dragged by mass, it was theorized that the mass of the sealed metal pipe would have been enough to cause a visible effect. Once again, no effect was seen, so aether-drag theories are considered to be disproven.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Walther_Ritz" title="Walther Ritz">Walther Ritz</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Emission_theory" title="Emission theory">emission theory</a> (or ballistic theory), was also consistent with the results of the experiment, not requiring aether. The theory postulates that light has always the same velocity in respect to the source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-norton_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-norton-40">[A 28]</a></sup> However <a href="/wiki/De_Sitter_double_star_experiment" title="De Sitter double star experiment">de Sitter</a> noted that emitter theory predicted several optical effects that were not seen in observations of binary stars in which the light from the two stars could be measured in a <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">spectrometer</a>. If emission theory were correct, the light from the stars should experience unusual fringe shifting due to the velocity of the stars being added to the speed of the light, but no such effect could be seen. It was later shown by <a href="/wiki/J._G._Fox" title="J. G. Fox">J. G. Fox</a> that the original de Sitter experiments were flawed due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinction_theorem_of_Ewald_and_Oseen" title="Extinction theorem of Ewald and Oseen">extinction</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fox65_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-fox65-41">[9]</a></sup> but in 1977 Brecher observed X-rays from binary star systems with similar null results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[10]</a></sup> Also terrestrial tests using <a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">particle accelerators</a> have been made that were inconsistent with source dependence of the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FilippasFox_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FilippasFox-43">[11]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Emission_theory" title="Emission theory">Emission theory</a> might fail the <a href="/wiki/Ives%E2%80%93Stilwell_experiment" title="Ives&#8211;Stilwell experiment">Ives&#8211;Stilwell experiment</a>, but Fox questioned that as well.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsequent_experiments">Subsequent experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Subsequent experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illingworth_simulation.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="395" data-file-width="436" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Illingworth_simulation.png/250px-Illingworth_simulation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Illingworth_simulation.png/375px-Illingworth_simulation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9c/Illingworth_simulation.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illingworth_simulation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Figure 8. Simulation of the Kennedy/Illingworth refinement of the Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment. (a) Michelson&#8211;Morley interference pattern in monochromatic mercury light, with a dark fringe precisely centered on the screen. (b) The fringes have been shifted to the left by 1/100 of the fringe spacing. It is extremely difficult to see any difference between this figure and the one above. (c) A small step in one mirror causes two views of the same fringes to be spaced 1/20 of the fringe spacing to the left and to the right of the step. (d) A telescope has been set to view only the central dark band around the mirror step. Note the symmetrical brightening about the center line. (e) The two sets of fringes have been shifted to the left by 1/100 of the fringe spacing. An abrupt discontinuity in luminosity is visible across the step.</div></div></div>\n<p>Although Michelson and Morley went on to different experiments after their first publication in 1887, both remained active in the field. Other versions of the experiment were carried out with increasing sophistication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swenson1_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swenson1-44">[A 29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swenson2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swenson2-45">[A 30]</a></sup> Morley was not convinced of his own results, and went on to conduct additional experiments with <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Miller" title="Dayton Miller">Dayton Miller</a> from 1902 to 1904. Again, the result was negative within the margins of error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morley1_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-morley1-46">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morley2_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-morley2-47">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Miller worked on increasingly larger interferometers, culminating in one with a 32-meter (105&#160;ft) (effective) arm length that he tried at various sites, including on top of a mountain at the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Mount Wilson Observatory</a>. To avoid the possibility of the aether wind being blocked by solid walls, his mountaintop observations used a special shed with thin walls, mainly of canvas. From noisy, irregular data, he consistently extracted a small positive signal that varied with each rotation of the device, with the <a href="/wiki/Sidereal_time" title="Sidereal time">sidereal day</a>, and on a yearly basis. His measurements in the 1920s amounted to approximately 10&#160;km/s (6.2&#160;mi/s) instead of the nearly 30&#160;km/s (18.6&#160;mi/s) expected from the Earth\'s orbital motion alone. He remained convinced this was due to <a href="/wiki/Aether_drag_hypothesis#Partial_aether_dragging" title="Aether drag hypothesis">partial entrainment or aether dragging</a>, though he did not attempt a detailed explanation. He ignored critiques demonstrating the inconsistency of his results and the refutation by the <a href="/wiki/Hammar_experiment" title="Hammar experiment">Hammar experiment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thirring_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thirring-48">[A 31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thirring_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thirring-49">[note 5]</a></sup> Miller\'s findings were considered important at the time, and were discussed by Michelson, <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">Lorentz</a> and others at a meeting reported in 1928.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-michel1928_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-michel1928-50">[A 32]</a></sup> There was general agreement that more experimentation was needed to check Miller\'s results. Miller later built a non-magnetic device to eliminate <a href="/wiki/Magnetostriction" title="Magnetostriction">magnetostriction</a>, while Michelson built one of non-expanding <a href="/wiki/Invar" title="Invar">Invar</a> to eliminate any remaining thermal effects. Other experimenters from around the world increased accuracy, eliminated possible side effects, or both. So far, no one has been able to replicate Miller\'s results, and modern experimental accuracies have ruled them out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shankland_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-shankland-51">[A 33]</a></sup> Roberts (2006) has pointed out that the primitive data reduction techniques used by Miller and other early experimenters, including Michelson and Morley, were capable of <i>creating</i> apparent periodic signals even when none existed in the actual data. After reanalyzing Miller\'s original data using modern techniques of quantitative error analysis, Roberts found Miller\'s apparent signals to be statistically insignificant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts2006_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts2006-52">[A 34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Using a special optical arrangement involving a 1/20 wave step in one mirror, Roy J. Kennedy (1926) and K.K. Illingworth (1927) (Fig.&#160;8) converted the task of detecting fringe shifts from the relatively insensitive one of estimating their lateral displacements to the considerably more sensitive task of adjusting the light intensity on both sides of a sharp boundary for equal luminance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kennedy_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kennedy-53">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Illingworth_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Illingworth-54">[15]</a></sup> If they observed unequal illumination on either side of the step, such as in Fig.&#160;8e, they would add or remove calibrated weights from the interferometer until both sides of the step were once again evenly illuminated, as in Fig.&#160;8d. The number of weights added or removed provided a measure of the fringe shift. Different observers could detect changes as little as 1/300 to 1/1500 of a fringe. Kennedy also carried out an experiment at Mount Wilson, finding only about 1/10 the drift measured by Miller and no seasonal effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-michel1928_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-michel1928-50">[A 32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1930, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Joos" title="Georg Joos">Georg Joos</a> conducted an experiment using an automated interferometer with 21-meter-long (69&#160;ft) arms forged from pressed quartz having very low thermal coefficient of expansion, that took continuous photographic strip recordings of the fringes through dozens of revolutions of the apparatus. Displacements of 1/1000 of a fringe could be measured on the photographic plates. No periodic fringe displacements were found, placing an upper limit to the aether wind of 1.5&#160;km/s (0.93&#160;mi/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joos_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-joos-55">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the table below, the expected values are related to the relative speed between Earth and Sun of 30&#160;km/s (18.6&#160;mi/s). With respect to the speed of the solar system around the galactic center of about 220&#160;km/s (140&#160;mi/s), or the speed of the solar system relative to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation#CMBR_dipole_anisotropy" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">CMB rest frame</a> of about 368&#160;km/s (229&#160;mi/s), the null results of those experiments are even more obvious.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Name\n</th>\n<th>Location\n</th>\n<th>Year\n</th>\n<th>Arm length (meters)\n</th>\n<th>Fringe shift expected\n</th>\n<th>Fringe shift measured\n</th>\n<th>Ratio\n</th>\n<th>Upper Limit on V<sub>aether</sub>\n</th>\n<th>Experimental Resolution\n</th>\n<th>Null result\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Michelson<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-michel1_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-michel1-13">[4]</a></sup></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Potsdam" title="Potsdam">Potsdam</a></td>\n<td>1881</td>\n<td>1.2</td>\n<td>0.04</td>\n<td>&#8804; Wilson</a></td>\n<td>1929</td>\n<td>25.9</td>\n<td>0.9</td>\n<td>&#8804; 0.01</td>\n<td>90</td>\n<td>&#8764; 3&#160;km/s</td>\n<td>0.01</td>\n<td>yes\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Georg_Joos" title="Georg Joos">Joos</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-joos_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-joos-55">[16]</a></sup></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Jena" title="Jena">Jena</a></td>\n<td>1930</td>\n<td>21.0</td>\n<td>0.75</td>\n<td>&#8804; 0.002</td>\n<td>375</td>\n<td>&#8764; 1.5&#160;km/s</td>\n<td>0.002</td>\n<td>yes\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_experiments">Recent experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Recent experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Optical_tests">Optical tests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Optical tests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Optical tests of the isotropy of the speed of light became commonplace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[A 35]</a></sup> New technologies, including the use of  <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">lasers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maser" title="Maser">masers</a>, have significantly improved measurement precision. (In the following table, only Essen (1955), Jaseja (1964), and Shamir/Fox (1969) are experiments of Michelson&#8211;Morley type, <i>i.e.</i> comparing two perpendicular beams. The other optical experiments employed different methods.)\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Author</th>\n<th>Year</th>\n<th>Description</th>\n<th>Upper bounds\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Louis_Essen" title="Louis Essen">Louis Essen</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-essen_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-essen-63">[23]</a></sup></td>\n<td>1955</td>\n<td>The frequency of a rotating microwave <a href="/wiki/Optical_cavity" title="Optical cavity">cavity resonator</a> is compared with that of a <a href="/wiki/Quartz_clock" title="Quartz clock">quartz clock</a></td>\n<td>~3&#160;km/s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Cedarholm <i>et al</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedarholm_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-cedarholm-64">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cedarholm2_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-cedarholm2-65">[25]</a></sup></td>\n<td>1958</td>\n<td>Two <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> masers were mounted on a rotating table, and their beams were directed in opposite directions.</td>\n<td>~30&#160;m/s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ives%E2%80%93Stilwell_experiment#M&#246;ssbauer_rotor_experiments" title="Ives&#8211;Stilwell experiment">M&#246;ssbauer rotor experiments</a></td>\n<td>1960&#8211;68</td>\n<td>In a series of experiments by different researchers, the frequencies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">gamma rays</a> were observed using the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ssbauer_effect" title="M&#246;ssbauer effect">M&#246;ssbauer effect</a>.</td>\n<td>~2.0&#160;cm/s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Jaseja <i>et al</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaseja_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jaseja-66">[26]</a></sup></td>\n<td>1964</td>\n<td>The frequencies of two <a href="/wiki/Helium%E2%80%93neon_laser" title="Helium&#8211;neon laser">He&#8211;Ne masers</a>, mounted on a rotating table, were compared. Unlike Cedarholm <i>et al.</i>, the masers were placed perpendicular to each other.</td>\n<td>~30&#160;m/s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td nowrap="nowrap">Shamir and Fox<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shamir_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-shamir-67">[27]</a></sup></td>\n<td>1969</td>\n<td>Both arms of the interferometer were contained in a transparent solid (<a href="/wiki/Poly(methyl_methacrylate)" title="Poly(methyl methacrylate)">plexiglass</a>). The light source was a <a href="/wiki/Helium%E2%80%93neon_laser" title="Helium&#8211;neon laser">Helium&#8211;neon laser</a>.</td>\n<td>~7&#160;km/s\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Trimmer <i>et al</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trimmer_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-trimmer-68">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trimmer2_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-trimmer2-69">[29]</a></sup></td>\n<td>1973</td>\n<td>They searched for anisotropies of the speed of light behaving as the first and third of the <a href="/wiki/Legendre_polynomials" title="Legendre polynomials">Legendre polynomials</a>. They used a triangle interferometer, with one portion of the path in glass. (In comparison, the Michelson&#8211;Morley type experiments test the second Legendre polynomial)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sexl_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-sexl-38">[A 27]</a></sup></td>\n<td>~2.5&#160;cm/s\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MMX_with_optical_resonators.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="448" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/MMX_with_optical_resonators.svg/250px-MMX_with_optical_resonators.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/MMX_with_optical_resonators.svg/375px-MMX_with_optical_resonators.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/MMX_with_optical_resonators.svg/500px-MMX_with_optical_resonators.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MMX_with_optical_resonators.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Figure 9. Michelson&#8211;Morley experiment with cryogenic optical resonators of a form such as was used by M&#252;ller <i>et al.</i> (2003).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muller2003_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muller2003-70">[30]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_optical_resonator_experiments">Recent optical resonator experiments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Recent optical resonator experiments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Over the last several years, there has been a resurgence in interest in performing precise Michelson&#8211;Morley type experiments using lasers, masers, cryogenic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_resonator" title="Optical resonator">optical resonators</a>, etc. This is in large part due to predictions of quantum gravity that suggest that special relativity may be violated at scales accessible to experimental study. The first of these highly accurate experiments was conducted by Brillet &amp; Hall (1979), in which they analyzed a laser frequency stabilized to a resonance of a rotating optical <a href="/wiki/Fabry%E2%80%93P%C3%A9rot_interferometer" title="Fabry&#8211;P&#233;rot interferometer">Fabry&#8211;P&#233;rot</a> cavity. They set a limit on the anisotropy of the speed of light resulting from the Earth\'s motions of &#916;<i>c</i>/<i>c</i>&#160;&#8776;&#160;10<sup>&#8722;15</sup>, where &#916;<i>c</i> is the difference between the speed of light in the <i>x</i>- and <i>y</i>-directions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brillet_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-brillet-71">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of 2009, optical and microwave resonator experiments have improved this limit to &#916;<i>c</i>/<i>c</i>&#160;&#8776;&#160;10<sup>&#8722;17</sup>. In some of them, the devices were rotated or remained stationary, and some were combined with the <a href="/wiki/Kennedy%E2%80%93Thorndike_experiment" title="Kennedy&#8211;Thorndike experiment">Kennedy&#8211;Thorndike experiment</a>. In particular, Earth\'s direction and velocity (ca.&#160;368&#160;km/s (229&#160;mi/s)) relative to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation#CMBR_dipole_anisotropy" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">CMB rest frame</a> are ordinarily used as references in these searches for anisotropies.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Author</th>\n<th>Year</th>\n<th>Description</th>\n<th>&#916;<i>c/c</i>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Wolf <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolf1_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-wolf1-72">[32]</a></sup></td>\n<td>2003</td>\n<td>The frequency of a stationary cryogenic microwave oscillator, consisting of sapphire crystal operating in a <a href="/wiki/Whispering-gallery_wave" title="Whispering-gallery wave">whispering gallery mode</a>, is compared to a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_maser" title="Hydrogen maser">hydrogen maser</a> whose frequency was compared to <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium</a> <a href="/wiki/Atomic_fountain" title="Atomic fountain">atomic fountain</a> clocks. Changes during Earth\'s rotation have been searched for. Data between 2001&#8211;2002 was analyzed.</td>\n<td rowspan="4"><center><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\lesssim 10^{-15}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo>&#8818;<!-- &#8818; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mn>10</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>15</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\lesssim 10^{-15}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\lesssim 10^{-15}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dec076b881fe8b73a7951a1bc8744dc1b61c8fa6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.933ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></center>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>M&#252;ller <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muller2003_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Muller2003-70">[30]</a></sup></td>\n<td>2003</td>\n<td>Two optical resonators constructed from crystalline sapphire, controlling the frequencies of two <a href="/wiki/Nd:YAG_laser" title="Nd:YAG laser">Nd:YAG lasers</a>, are set at right angles within a helium cryostat. A frequency comparator measures the beat frequency of the combined outputs of the two resonators.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Wolf <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wolf2_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-wolf2-73">[33]</a></sup></td>\n<td>2004</td>\n<td>See Wolf <i>et al.</i> (2003). An active temperature control was implemented. Data between 2002&#8211;2004 was analyzed.</td>\n<td rowspan="5"><center><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\lesssim 10^{-16}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo>&#8818;<!-- &#8818; --></mo>\n<msup>\n<mn>10</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>16</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\lesssim 10^{-16}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\lesssim 10^{-16}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3146943be6491284683490f921955f387410e306" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.933ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></center>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Stanwix <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanwix_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanwix-76">[36]</a></sup></td>\n<td>2005</td>\n<td>Similar to Wolf <i>et al.</i> (2003). Data between 2004&#8211;2005 was analyzed.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Stanwix <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanwix2_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanwix2-78">[38]</a></sup></td>\n<td>2006</td>\n<td>See Stanwix <i>et al.</i> (2005).
